	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Citizens United v. FEC</i></h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Citizens United" redirects here. For the political organization, see <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_(organization)" title="Citizens United (organization)">Citizens United (organization)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Citizens United (disambiguation)">Citizens United (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</p>

Ct. 876; 175 <a href="/wiki/L._Ed._2d" class="mw-redirect" title="L. Ed. 2d">L. United States District Court for the District of Columbia reversed.</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #99c0ff; white-space:nowrap">Court membership</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><dl style="margin:0; padding:0.1em 0.5em; text-align:center; line-height:1.3em;">

Stevens</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b>&#32; <a href="/wiki/Antonin_Scalia" title="Antonin Scalia">Antonin Scalia</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kennedy" title="Anthony Kennedy">Anthony Kennedy</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b>&#32; <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Clarence Thomas</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Ruth_Bader_Ginsburg" title="Ruth Bader Ginsburg">Ruth Bader Ginsburg</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b>&#32; <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Breyer" title="Stephen Breyer">Stephen Breyer</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Alito" title="Samuel Alito">Samuel Alito</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b>&#32; <a href="/wiki/Sonia_Sotomayor" title="Sonia Sotomayor">Sonia Sotomayor</a>
</dd></dl></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #99c0ff; white-space:nowrap">Case opinions</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Majority</th><td>Kennedy, joined by Roberts, Scalia, Alito (in full); Thomas (all but Part IV); Stevens, Ginsburg, Breyer, Sotomayor (only as to Part IV)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Concurrence</th><td>Roberts, joined by Alito</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Concurrence</th><td>Scalia, joined by Alito; Thomas (in part)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Concur/dissent</th><td>Stevens, joined by Ginsburg, Breyer, Sotomayor</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Concur/dissent</th><td>Thomas</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #99c0ff; white-space:nowrap">Laws applied</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">U.S. Const. amend. I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div style="background-color: green; color: white; font-weight: bold">This case overturned a previous ruling or rulings</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><i><a href="/wiki/Austin_v._Michigan_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce">Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce</a></i>, 1990. <i><a href="/wiki/McConnell_v._FEC" title="McConnell v. FEC">McConnell v. FEC</a></i>, 2003 (in part).</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>Citizens United v. Federal Election Commission</b></i>, 558 U.S. 310 (2010), is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_landmark_court_decisions_in_the_United_States" title="List of landmark court decisions in the United States">landmark</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> case concerning <a href="/wiki/Campaign_finance_in_the_United_States" title="Campaign finance in the United States">campaign finance</a>. The Court held that the <a href="/wiki/Free_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Free speech">free speech</a> clause of the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a> prohibits the government from restricting <a href="/wiki/Independent_expenditure" title="Independent expenditure">independent expenditures</a> for political communications by <a href="/wiki/Corporations" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporations">corporations</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit_corporation" title="Nonprofit corporation">nonprofit corporations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor unions</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_association" title="Voluntary association">associations</a>.
</p><p>The case arose after <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_(organization)" title="Citizens United (organization)">Citizens United</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit_organization" title="Nonprofit organization">non-profit organization</a>, sought to air and advertise a film critical of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> shortly before the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 2008">2008 Democratic primary elections</a>. This would have been a violation of the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act</a>, which prohibited any corporation or labor union from making an "electioneering communication" within 30 days of a primary or 60 days of an election, or making any expenditure advocating the election or defeat of a candidate at any time.
</p><p>In a majority opinion joined by four other justices, Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kennedy" title="Anthony Kennedy">Anthony Kennedy</a> held that the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act's prohibition of all independent expenditures by corporations and unions violated the First Amendment's protection of free speech. The Court overruled <i><a href="/wiki/Austin_v._Michigan_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce">Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce</a></i> (1990), which had allowed different restrictions on speech-related spending based on corporate identity, as well as a portion of <i><a href="/wiki/McConnell_v._FEC" title="McConnell v. FEC">McConnell v. FEC</a></i> (2003) that had restricted corporate spending on electioneering communications. The ruling effectively freed labor unions and corporations to spend money on electioneering communications and to directly advocate for the election or defeat of candidates. In his dissenting opinion, Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_Stevens" title="John Paul Stevens">John Paul Stevens</a> argued that Court's ruling represented "a rejection of the common sense of the American people, who have recognized a need to prevent corporations from undermining self government."
</p><p>The decision remains highly controversial, generating much public discussion and receiving strong support and opposition from various groups. Senator <a href="/wiki/Mitch_McConnell" title="Mitch McConnell">Mitch McConnell</a> commended the decision, arguing that it represented "an important step in the direction of restoring the First Amendment rights". By contrast, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> stated that the decision "gives the special interests and their lobbyists even more power in Washington". The ruling had a major impact on campaign finance, allowing unlimited election spending by corporations and labor unions and fueling the rise of <a href="/wiki/Super_PACs" class="mw-redirect" title="Super PACs">Super PACs</a>. Later rulings by the <a href="/wiki/Roberts_Court" title="Roberts Court">Roberts Court</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/McCutcheon_v._FEC" title="McCutcheon v. FEC">McCutcheon v. FEC</a></i> (2014), would strike down other campaign finance restrictions.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Case_summary">Case summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Case summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the case, No. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/558/08-205/">08-205</a>,&#32;<a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_558" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 558">558</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/558/310/">310</a>&#32;(2010),
the <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit_organization" title="Nonprofit organization">non-profit organization</a> <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_(organization)" title="Citizens United (organization)">Citizens United</a> wanted to air a film critical of <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> and to advertise the film during television broadcasts, which was a violation of the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act</a>, commonly known as the McCain–Feingold Act or "BCRA" (pronounced "bik-ruh").<sup id="cite_ref-LiptakJan2010_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiptakJan2010-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Section 203 of BCRA defined an "electioneering communication" as a broadcast, cable, or satellite communication that mentioned a candidate within 60 days of a general election or 30 days of a primary, and prohibited such expenditures by corporations and unions. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_Columbia" title="United States District Court for the District of Columbia">United States District Court for the District of Columbia</a> held that §203 of BCRA applied and prohibited Citizens United from advertising the film <i><a href="/wiki/Hillary:_The_Movie" title="Hillary: The Movie">Hillary: The Movie</a></i> in broadcasts or paying to have it shown on television within 30 days of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)_presidential_primaries,_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Party (United States) presidential primaries, 2008">2008 Democratic primaries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Liptak2009_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liptak2009-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The Supreme Court reversed this decision, striking down those provisions of BCRA that prohibited corporations (including nonprofit corporations) and unions from making independent expenditures for "electioneering communications".<sup id="cite_ref-LiptakJan2010_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiptakJan2010-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The majority decision overruled <i><a href="/wiki/Austin_v._Michigan_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce">Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce</a></i> (1990) and partially overruled <i><a href="/wiki/McConnell_v._Federal_Election_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="McConnell v. Federal Election Commission">McConnell v. Federal Election Commission</a></i> (2003).<sup id="cite_ref-Hasen2010_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasen2010-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  The Court, however, upheld requirements for public disclosure by sponsors of advertisements (BCRA §201 and §311). The case did not involve the federal ban on direct contributions from corporations or unions to candidate campaigns or political parties, which remain illegal in races for federal office.<sup id="cite_ref-Carney2010_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carney2010-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Section 203 of the <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act</a> of 2002 (known as BCRA or McCain–Feingold Act) modified the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Campaign_Act" title="Federal Election Campaign Act">Federal Election Campaign Act</a> of 1971, <a href="/wiki/Title_2_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 2 of the United States Code">2 U.S.C.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/2/441b">§&#160;441b</a> to prohibit corporations and unions from using their general treasury to fund "electioneering communications" (broadcast advertisements mentioning a candidate in any context) within 30 days before a primary or 60 days before a general election.  During the 2004 presidential campaign, <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_(organization)" title="Citizens United (organization)">Citizens United</a>, a nonprofit <a href="/wiki/501(c)(4)" class="mw-redirect" title="501(c)(4)">501(c)(4)</a> organization, filed a complaint before the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission" title="Federal Election Commission">Federal Election Commission</a> (FEC) charging that advertisements for <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore's</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit_9/11" title="Fahrenheit 9/11">Fahrenheit 9/11</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Docudrama" title="Docudrama">docudrama</a> critical of the <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">Bush administration's</a> response to the <a href="/wiki/9/11" class="mw-redirect" title="9/11">terrorist attacks on September 11, 2001</a>, produced and marketed by a variety of corporate entities, constituted political advertising and thus could not be aired within the 30 days before a primary election or 60 days before a general election.  The FEC dismissed the complaint after finding no evidence that broadcast advertisements featuring a candidate within the proscribed time limits had actually been made.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The FEC later dismissed a second complaint which argued that the movie itself constituted illegal corporate spending advocating the election or defeat of a candidate, which was illegal under the <a href="/wiki/Taft-Hartley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Taft-Hartley Act">Taft-Hartley Act</a> of 1947 and the Federal Election Campaign Act Amendments of 1974. In dismissing that complaint, the FEC found that:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The complainant alleged that the release and distribution of FAHRENHEIT 9/11 constituted an independent expenditure because the film expressly advocated the defeat of President George W. Bush and that by being fully or partially responsible for the film's release, Michael Moore and other entities associated with the film (made by Nuss &amp; co.) excessive and/or prohibited contributions to unidentified candidates. The Commission found no reason to believe the respondents violated the Act because the film, associated trailers and website represented bona fide commercial activity, not "contributions" or "expenditures" as defined by the Federal Election Campaign Act.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In response, Citizens United produced the documentary <i>Celsius 41.11</i>, which is highly critical of both <i>Fahrenheit 9/11 </i>and 2004 Democratic presidential nominee John Kerry. The FEC, however, held that showing the movie and advertisements for it would violate the Federal Election Campaign Act, because Citizens United was not a bona fide commercial film maker.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the wake of these decisions, Citizens United sought to establish itself as a bona fide commercial film maker before the 2008 elections, producing several documentary films between 2005 and 2007. By early 2008, it sought to run television commercials to promote its political documentary <i>Hillary: The Movie</i> and to air the movie on <a href="/wiki/DirecTV" title="DirecTV">DirecTV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_District_Court">In the District Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: In the District Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In December 2007, Citizens United filed a complaint in U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia challenging the constitutionality of several statutory provisions governing "electioneering communications".<sup id="cite_ref-FECL_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FECL-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It asked the court to declare that the prohibition on corporate and union funding were facially unconstitutional, and also as applied to <i>Hillary: The Movie,</i> and to enjoin the Federal Election Commission from enforcing its regulations. Citizens United also argued that the Commission's disclosure and disclaimer requirements were unconstitutional as applied to the movie pursuant to the Supreme Court decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission_v._Wisconsin_Right_to_Life,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Election Commission v. Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc.">Federal Election Commission v. Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc.</a></i>.  It also sought to enjoin funding, disclosure and disclaimer requirements as applied to Citizens United's intended ads for the movie.
</p><p>In accordance with special rules in section 403 of the <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">BCRA</a>, a three-judge court was convened to hear the case. On January 15, 2008, the court denied Citizens United's motion for a preliminary injunction, finding that the suit had little chance of success because the movie had no reasonable interpretation other than as an appeal to vote against Senator Clinton, that it was therefore express advocacy, not entitled to exemption from the ban on corporate funding of electioneering communications, and that television advertisements for the movie within 30 days of a primary violated the BCRA restrictions on "electioneering communications".<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The court held that the Supreme Court in <i><a href="/wiki/McConnell_v._FEC" title="McConnell v. FEC">McConnell v. FEC</a></i> (2003) had found the disclosure requirements constitutional as to all electioneering communications, and <i>Wisconsin RTL</i> did not disturb this holding because the only issue of that case was whether speech that did not constitute the functional equivalent of express advocacy could be banned during the relevant pre-election period.
</p><p>On July 18, 2008, the District Court granted summary judgment to the Federal Election Commission. In accordance with the special rules in the BCRA, Citizens United appealed to the Supreme Court which docketed the case on August 18, 2008 and granted certiorari on November 14, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008a_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008a-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Supreme Court heard oral argument on March 24, 2009<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ross_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holland_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holland-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and then asked for further briefs on June 29; the re-argument was heard on September 9, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008a_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008a-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_the_Supreme_Court">Before the Supreme Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Before the Supreme Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During the original <a href="/wiki/Oral_argument_in_the_United_States" title="Oral argument in the United States">oral argument</a>, Deputy <a href="/wiki/Solicitor_General_of_the_United_States" title="Solicitor General of the United States">Solicitor General</a> Malcolm L. Stewart (representing the FEC) argued that under <i><a href="/wiki/Austin_v._Michigan_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce">Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce</a></i>, the government would have the power to ban books if those books contained even one sentence expressly advocating the election or defeat of a candidate and were published or distributed by a corporation or labor union.<sup id="cite_ref-Liptak2009a_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liptak2009a-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>  In response to this line of questioning, Stewart further argued that under <i>Austin</i> the government could ban the digital distribution of political books over the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Kindle" title="Amazon Kindle">Amazon Kindle</a> or prevent a union from hiring an author to write a political book.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2012 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Toobin" title="Jeffrey Toobin">Jeffrey Toobin</a>, the Court expected after oral argument to rule on the narrow question that had originally been presented—Can Citizens United show the film? At the subsequent conference among the justices after oral argument, the vote was 5–4 in favor of Citizens United being allowed to show the film. The justices voted the same as they had in <i><a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission_v._Wisconsin_Right_to_Life,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Election Commission v. Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc.">Federal Election Commission v. Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc.</a></i>, a similar 2007 case, with Chief Justice Roberts and Justices Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas and Alito in the majority.<sup id="cite_ref-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/John_Roberts" title="John Roberts">John Roberts</a> wrote the initial opinion of the Court, <a href="/wiki/Holding_(law)" title="Holding (law)">holding</a> that the BCRA allowed the showing of the film. A draft <a href="/wiki/Concurring_opinion" title="Concurring opinion">concurring opinion</a> by Justice Kennedy argued that the court could and should have gone much further. The other justices in the majority agreed with Kennedy's reasoning, and convinced Roberts to reassign the writing and allow Kennedy's concurrence to become the majority opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other side, <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_Stevens" title="John Paul Stevens">John Paul Stevens</a>, the most senior justice in the minority, assigned the <a href="/wiki/Dissenting_opinion" title="Dissenting opinion">dissent</a> to <a href="/wiki/David_Souter" title="David Souter">David Souter</a>, who announced his retirement from the Court while he was working on it. The final draft went beyond critiquing the majority. Toobin described it as "air[ing] some of the Court's dirty laundry," writing that Souter's dissent accused Roberts of having manipulated Court procedures to reach his desired result—an expansive decision that, Souter claimed, changed decades of election law and ruled on issues neither party to the litigation had presented.<sup id="cite_ref-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Toobin, Roberts agreed to withdraw the opinion and schedule the case for reargument. When he did, the "Questions Presented" to the parties were, however, more expansive, touching on the issues Kennedy's opinion had identified. According to Toobin, the eventual result was therefore a foregone conclusion from that point on.<sup id="cite_ref-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toobin_2012_New_Yorker_article-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Toobin's account has been criticized for drawing conclusions unsupported by the evidence in his article.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeff_Toobin_on_Citizens_United_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeff_Toobin_on_Citizens_United-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 29, 2009, the last day of the term, the Court issued an order directing the parties to re-argue the case on September 9 after briefing whether it might be necessary to overrule <i>Austin</i> and/or <i><a href="/wiki/McConnell_v._Federal_Election_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="McConnell v. Federal Election Commission">McConnell v. Federal Election Commission</a></i> to decide the case.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2009_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2009-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Justice Stevens noted in his dissent that in its prior motion for summary judgment, Citizens United had abandoned its <a href="/wiki/Facial_challenge" title="Facial challenge">facial challenge</a> of BCRA §203's constitutionality, with the parties agreeing to the dismissal of the claim.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sonia_Sotomayor" title="Sonia Sotomayor">Justice Sotomayor</a> sat on the bench for the first time during the second round of oral arguments. This was the first case argued by then-Solicitor General and future Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Elena_Kagan" title="Elena Kagan">Elena Kagan</a>. Former Bush Solicitor General <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Olson" title="Theodore Olson">Ted Olson</a> and First Amendment lawyer <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Abrams" title="Floyd Abrams">Floyd Abrams</a> argued for Citizens United, and former Clinton Solicitor General <a href="/wiki/Seth_Waxman" class="mw-redirect" title="Seth Waxman">Seth Waxman</a> defended the statute on behalf of various supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_a_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_a-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Legal scholar <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Chemerinsky" title="Erwin Chemerinsky">Erwin Chemerinsky</a> called it "one of the most important First Amendment cases in years".<sup id="cite_ref-Liptak2009b_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liptak2009b-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decision">Decision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Decision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On January 21, 2010, the Court issued a 5–4 decision in favor of Citizens United that struck down the BCRA's restrictions on independent expenditures from corporate treasures as violations of the First Amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChemerinsky2019§&amp;nbsp;11.3.6.3,_p._1184_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChemerinsky2019§&amp;nbsp;11.3.6.3,_p._1184-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinion_of_the_Court">Opinion of the Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Opinion of the Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg/175px-Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg" decoding="async" width="175" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg/263px-Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg/350px-Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1396" data-file-height="1745" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthony_Kennedy_official_SCOTUS_portrait_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Justice Kennedy, the author of the Court's opinion</div></div></div>
<p>Five justices formed the majority and joined an opinion written by Justice <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kennedy" title="Anthony Kennedy">Anthony Kennedy</a>. The Court found that the BCRA §203 prohibition of all independent expenditures by corporations and unions violated the First Amendment's protection of free speech.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The majority wrote, "If the First Amendment has any force, it prohibits Congress from fining or jailing citizens, or associations of citizens, for simply engaging in political speech."<sup id="cite_ref-TNY20120521_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TNY20120521-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Justice Kennedy's opinion also noted that because the First Amendment does not distinguish between media and other corporations, the BCRA restrictions improperly allowed Congress to suppress political speech in newspapers, books, television, and blogs.<sup id="cite_ref-LiptakJan2010_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiptakJan2010-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The Court overruled <i>Austin</i>, which had held that a state law that prohibited corporations from using treasury money to support or oppose candidates in elections did not violate the First and Fourteenth Amendments. The Court also overruled that portion of <i>McConnell</i> that upheld BCRA's restriction of corporate spending on "electioneering communications". The Court's ruling effectively freed corporations and unions to spend money both on "electioneering communications" and to directly advocate for the election or defeat of candidates (although not to contribute directly to candidates or political parties).
</p><p>The majority ruled that the Freedom of the Press clause of the First Amendment protects <i>associations</i> of individuals in addition to individual speakers, and further that the First Amendment does not allow prohibitions of speech based on the identity of the speaker. Corporations, as associations of individuals, therefore have free speech rights under the First Amendment. Because spending money is essential to disseminating speech, as established in <i><a href="/wiki/Buckley_v._Valeo" title="Buckley v. Valeo">Buckley v. Valeo</a></i>, limiting a corporation's ability to spend money is unconstitutional because it limits the ability of its members to associate effectively and to speak on political issues.
</p><p>The decision overruled <i>Austin</i> because that decision allowed different restrictions on speech-related spending based on corporate identity.  Additionally, the decision said that <i>Austin</i> was based on an "equality" rationale—trying to equalize speech between different speakers—that the Court had previously rejected as illegitimate under the First Amendment in <i>Buckley</i>. The Michigan statute at issue in <i>Austin</i> had distinguished between corporate and union spending, prohibiting the former while allowing the latter. The <i>Austin</i> Court, over the dissent by Justices Scalia, Kennedy, and O'Connor, had held that such distinctions were within the legislature's prerogative. In <i>Citizens United v. Federal Election Commission</i>, however, the majority argued that the First Amendment purposefully keeps the government from interfering in the "marketplace of ideas" and "rationing" speech, and it is not up to the legislatures or the courts to create a sense of "fairness" by restricting speech.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority also criticized <i>Austin's</i> reasoning that the "distorting effect" of large corporate expenditures constituted a risk of corruption or the <a href="/wiki/Appearance_of_corruption" title="Appearance of corruption">appearance of corruption</a>. Rather, the majority argued that the government had no place in determining whether large expenditures distorted an audience's perceptions, and that the type of "corruption" that might justify government controls on spending for speech had to relate to some form of "quid pro quo" transaction: "There is no such thing as too much speech."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  The public has a right to have access to all information and to determine the reliability and importance of the information. Additionally, the majority did not believe that reliable evidence substantiated the risk of corruption or the appearance of corruption, and so this rationale did not satisfy <a href="/wiki/Strict_scrutiny" title="Strict scrutiny">strict scrutiny</a>.
</p><p>The Court's opinion relied heavily on the reasoning and principles of the landmark campaign finance case of <i>Buckley</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/First_National_Bank_of_Boston_v._Bellotti" title="First National Bank of Boston v. Bellotti">First National Bank of Boston v. Bellotti</a></i>, in which the Court struck down a broad prohibition against independent expenditures by corporations in ballot initiatives and referenda.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Specifically, the Court echoed <i>Bellotti's</i> rejection of categories based on a corporation's purpose.  The majority argued that to grant Freedom of the Press protections to media corporations, but not others, presented a host of problems; and so all corporations should be equally protected from expenditure restrictions.
</p><p>
The Court found that BCRA §§201 and 311, provisions requiring disclosure of the funder, were valid as applied to the movie advertisements and to the movie itself.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The majority ruled for the disclosure of the sources of campaign contributions, saying that</p><blockquote><p>... prompt disclosure of expenditures can provide shareholders and citizens with the information needed to hold corporations and elected officials accountable for their positions and supporters. Shareholders can determine whether their corporation’s political speech advances the corporation’s interest in making profits, and citizens can see whether elected officials are "in the pocket" of so-called moneyed interests... This transparency enables the electorate to make informed decisions and give proper weight to different speakers and messages.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concurrences">Concurrences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Concurrences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_Roberts" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Justice Roberts">Chief Justice Roberts</a>, with whom <a href="/wiki/Justice_Alito" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Alito">Justice Alito</a> joined, wrote separately "to address the important principles of <a href="/wiki/Judicial_restraint" title="Judicial restraint">judicial restraint</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Stare_decisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Stare decisis">stare decisis</a></i> implicated in this case".<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_b_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_b-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roberts wrote to further explain and defend the Court's statement that "there is a difference between judicial restraint and judicial abdication."  Roberts explained why the Court must sometimes overrule prior decisions. Had prior Courts never gone against <i>stare decisis</i> (that is, against precedent), for example, "segregation would be legal, minimum wage laws would be unconstitutional, and the Government could wiretap ordinary criminal suspects without first obtaining warrants".  Roberts's concurrence recited a plethora of case law in which the court had ruled against precedent.  Ultimately, Roberts argued that "<i>stare decisis</i>... counsels deference to past mistakes, but provides no justification for making new ones".<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_b_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_b-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Justice_Scalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Scalia">Justice Scalia</a> joined the opinion of the Court, and wrote a concurring opinion which <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Alito" title="Samuel Alito">Justice Alito</a> joined in full and <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Justice Thomas</a> joined in part. Scalia addressed <a href="/wiki/Justice_Stevens" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Stevens">Justice Stevens</a>' dissent, specifically with regard to the <a href="/wiki/Original_understanding" class="mw-redirect" title="Original understanding">original understanding</a> of the First Amendment. Scalia wrote that Stevens's dissent was "in splendid isolation from the text of the First Amendment... It never shows why 'the freedom of speech' that was the right of Englishmen did not include the freedom to speak in association with other individuals, including association in the corporate form."  He further considered the dissent's exploration of the Framers' views about the "role of corporations in society" to be misleading, and even if valid, irrelevant to the text. Scalia principally argued that the First Amendment was written in "terms of speech, not speakers" and that "Its text offers no foothold for excluding any category of speaker."<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_c_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_c-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  Scalia argued that the Free Press clause was originally intended to protect the distribution of written materials and did not only apply to the media specifically.  This understanding supported the majority's contention that the Constitution does not allow the Court to separate corporations into media and non-media categories.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Justice Thomas</a> wrote a separate opinion concurring in all but the upholding of the disclosure provisions. In order to protect the anonymity of contributors to organizations exercising free speech, Thomas would have struck down the reporting requirements of BCRA&#160;§201 and §311 as well, rather than allowing them to be challenged only on a case-specific basis. Thomas's primary argument was that anonymous free speech is protected and that making contributor lists public makes the contributors vulnerable to retaliation, citing instances of retaliation against contributors to both sides of a then-recent California voter initiative. Thomas also expressed concern that such retaliation could extend to retaliation by elected officials. Thomas did not consider "as-applied challenges" to be sufficient to protect against the threat of retaliation.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_e_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_e-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissent">Dissent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dissent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Paul_Stevens,_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/John_Paul_Stevens%2C_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg/175px-John_Paul_Stevens%2C_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="175" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/John_Paul_Stevens%2C_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg/263px-John_Paul_Stevens%2C_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/John_Paul_Stevens%2C_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg/350px-John_Paul_Stevens%2C_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2392" data-file-height="2377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Paul_Stevens,_SCOTUS_photo_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Justice Stevens, the author of the dissenting opinion</div></div></div>
<p>A dissenting opinion by <a href="/wiki/Justice_Stevens" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Stevens">Justice Stevens</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ref_d_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_d-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> was joined by <a href="/wiki/Justice_Ginsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Ginsburg">Justice Ginsburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justice_Breyer" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Breyer">Justice Breyer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Justice_Sotomayor" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Sotomayor">Justice Sotomayor</a>. To emphasize his unhappiness with the majority, Stevens read part of his 90-page dissent from the bench.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Stevens concurred in the Court's decision to sustain BCRA's disclosure provisions but dissented from the principal holding of the Court. He argued that the Court's ruling "threatens to undermine the integrity of elected institutions across the Nation. The path it has taken to reach its outcome will, I fear, do damage to this institution." He added: "A democracy cannot function effectively when its constituent members believe laws are being bought and sold."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Stevens also argued that the Court addressed a question not raised by the litigants when it found BCRA&#160;§203 to be facially unconstitutional, and that the majority "changed the case to give themselves an opportunity to change the law".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He argued that the majority had expanded the scope beyond the questions presented by the appellant and that therefore a sufficient record for judging the case did not exist. Stevens argued that at a minimum the Court should have remanded the case for a fact-finding hearing, and that the majority did not consider other compilations of data, such as the Congressional record for justifying BCRA §203.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Corruption_concerns">Corruption concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Corruption concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Stevens argued that the Court had long recognized that to deny Congress the power to safeguard against "the improper use of money to influence the result [of an election] is to deny to the nation in a vital particular the power of self protection".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  After recognizing that in <i>Buckley v. Valeo</i> the Court had struck down portions of a broad prohibition of independent expenditures from any sources, Stevens argued that nevertheless <i>Buckley</i> recognized the legitimacy of "prophylactic" measures for limiting campaign spending and found the prevention of "corruption" to be a reasonable goal for legislation.  Consequently, Stevens argued that <i>Buckley</i> left the door open for carefully tailored future regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  Although the majority echoed many of the arguments in <i>First National Bank of Boston v. Bellotti</i>, Stevens argued that the majority opinion contradicted the reasoning of other campaign finance cases—in particular, <i>Austin v. Michigan State Chamber of Commerce</i> and <i>McConnell v. Federal Election Commission</i>—and found it telling that the majority, when citing such cases, referenced mainly dissenting opinions.
</p><p>Stevens argued that the majority failed to recognize the possibility for corruption outside strict <i>quid pro quo</i> exchanges.  He referenced facts from a previous BCRA challenge to argue that, even if the exchange of votes for expenditures could not be shown, contributors gain favorable political access from such expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The majority, however, had considered access to be insufficient justification for limiting speech rights.
</p><p>Stevens responded that in the past, even when striking down a ban on corporate independent expenditures, the Court "never suggested that such quid pro quo debts must take the form of outright vote buying or bribes" (<i>Bellotti</i>). <i>Buckley</i>, he said, also acknowledged that large independent expenditures present the same dangers as <i>quid pro quo</i> arrangements, although <i>Buckley</i> struck down limits on such independent expenditures. Using the record from a previous BCRA §203 challenge, he argued that independent expenditures were sometimes a factor in gaining political access and concluded that large independent expenditures generate <i>more</i> influence than direct campaign contributions.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  Furthermore, Stevens argued that corporations could threaten Representatives and Senators with negative advertising to gain unprecedented leverage. Stevens supported his argument by citing <i><a href="/wiki/Caperton_v._A.T._Massey_Coal_Co." title="Caperton v. A.T. Massey Coal Co.">Caperton v. A.T. Massey Coal Co.</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> where the Court held that $3 million in independent expenditures in a judicial race raised sufficient questions about a judge's impartiality to require the judge to recuse himself in a future case involving the spender. Stevens argued that it was contradictory for the majority to ignore the same risks in legislative and executive elections, and argued that the majority opinion would exacerbate the problem presented in <i>Caperton</i> because of the number of states with judicial elections and increased spending in judicial races.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_appearance_of_corruption">The appearance of corruption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The appearance of corruption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Second, Stevens argued that the majority did not place enough emphasis on the need to prevent the "appearance of corruption" in elections.  Earlier cases, including <i>Buckley</i> and <i>Bellotti</i>, recognized the importance of public confidence in democracy.  Stevens cited recent data indicating that 80% of the public view corporate independent expenditures as a method used to gain unfair legislative access.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  Stevens predicted that if the public believes that corporations dominate elections, disaffected voters will stop participating.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporations_as_part_of_the_political_process">Corporations as part of the political process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Corporations as part of the political process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Third, Stevens argued that the majority's decision failed to recognize the dangers of the corporate form. <i>Austin</i> held that the prevention of corruption, including the distorting influence of a dominant funding source, was a sufficient reason for regulating corporate independent expenditures. In defending <i>Austin</i>, Stevens argued that the unique qualities of corporations and other artificial legal entities made them dangerous to democratic elections. These legal entities, he argued, have perpetual life, the ability to amass large sums of money, limited liability, no ability to vote, no morality, no purpose outside profit-making, and no loyalty. Therefore, he argued, the courts should permit legislatures to regulate corporate participation in the political process.
</p><p>Legal entities, Stevens wrote, are not "We the People" for whom our Constitution was established.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, he argued, they should not be given speech protections under the First Amendment. The First Amendment, he argued, protects individual self-expression, self-realization and the communication of ideas. Corporate spending is the "furthest from the core of political expression" protected by the Constitution, he argued, citing <i>Federal Election Commission v. Beaumont</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and corporate spending on politics should be viewed as a business transaction designed by the officers or the <a href="/wiki/Boards_of_directors" class="mw-redirect" title="Boards of directors">boards of directors</a> for no purpose other than profit-making.  Stevens called corporate spending "more transactional than ideological".  Stevens also pointed out that any member of a corporation may spend personal money on promoting a campaign because BCRA only prohibited the use of general treasury money.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_speech_concerns">Freedom of speech concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Freedom of speech concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Stevens critiqued the majority's main argument: the prohibition of spending guards free speech and allows the general public to receive all available information. Citing <i>Austin</i>, Stevens argued that corporations unfairly influence the electoral process with vast sums of money that few individuals can match.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  This process, he argued, puts disproportionate focus on supporting this type of speech and gives the impression of widespread acclaim regardless of actual support.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_censorship_concerns">Media censorship concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Media censorship concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fifth, Stevens criticized the majority's fear that the government could use BCRA §203 to censor the media. The focus placed on this hypothetical fear made no sense to him because it did not relate to the facts of this case—if the government actually attempted to apply BCRA §203 to the media (and assuming that Citizens United could not constitute media), the Court could deal with the problem at that time. Stevens described the majority's supposed protection of the media as nothing more than posturing. According to him, it was the majority's new rule in this case, that prohibited a law from distinguishing between speakers or funding sources. This new rule would be the only reason why media corporations could not be exempted from BCRA §203.
</p><p>Stevens recognizes that "[t]he press plays a unique role not only in the text, history, and structure of the First Amendment but also in facilitating public discourse,<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_d_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_d-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>" and even grants that the majority "raised some interesting and difficult questions about Congress’ authority to regulate electioneering by the press, and about how to define what constitutes the press."   In response he forcefully indicates that, in his view, (emphasis in original) "<i>that [this question of regulating and defining the press] is not the case before us.</i>"  Stevens opinion expresses his view that the institutional press can be distinguished from other persons and entities that are not the press while the majority opinion viewed "freedom of the press" as an activity, applicable to all citizens or groups of citizens seeking to publish views.  However, while Stevens has been interpreted as implying the press clause specifically protects the institutional press it isn't clear from his opinion.  In footnote 62 Stevens does argue that the free press clause demonstrates "that the drafters of the First Amendment did draw distinctions—explicit distinctions—between types of “speakers,” or speech outlets or forms" but the disjunctive form of the sentence doesn't clearly entail that the distinction must have been between types of speakers rather than outlets or forms.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Losing_faith_in_our_democracy">Losing faith in our democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Losing faith in our democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sixth, Stevens claimed that the majority failed to give proper deference to the legislature.  Stevens predicted that this ruling would restrict the ability of the states to experiment with different methods for decreasing corruption in elections.  According to Stevens, this ruling virtually ended those efforts, "declaring by fiat" that people will not "lose faith in our democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Stevens argued that the majority's view of a self-serving legislature, passing campaign-spending laws to gain an advantage in retaining a seat, coupled with "strict scrutiny" of laws, would make it difficult for any campaign finance regulation to be upheld in future cases.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shareholders_rights">Shareholders rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Shareholders rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Seventh, Stevens argued that the majority opinion ignored the rights of shareholders.  A series of cases protects individuals from legally compelled payment of union dues to support political speech.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  Because shareholders invest money in corporations, Stevens argued that the law should likewise help to protect shareholders from funding speech that they oppose.  The majority, however, argued that ownership of corporate stock was voluntary and that unhappy shareholders could simply sell off their shares if they did not agree with the corporation's speech. Stevens also argued that  <a href="/wiki/Political_Action_Committees" class="mw-redirect" title="Political Action Committees">Political Action Committees</a> (PACs), which allow individual members of a corporation to invest money in a separate fund, are an adequate substitute for general corporate speech and better protect shareholder rights. The majority, by contrast, had argued that most corporations are too small and lack the resources and raw number of shareholders and management staff necessary to maintain compliance, accounting and administrative costs of a PAC. In this dispute, the opposing views essentially discussed differing types of entities: Stevens focused his argument on large, publicly held corporations while the justices in the majority, particularly Justice Scalia's concurring opinion, placed an emphasis on small, closely held corporations and non-profits.
</p><p>Stevens called the majority's faith in "corporate democracy" an unrealistic method for a shareholder to oppose political funding.  A <a href="/wiki/Derivative_suit" title="Derivative suit">derivative suit</a> is slow, inefficient, risky and potentially expensive.  Likewise, shareholder meetings only happen a few times a year, not prior to every decision or transaction.  Rather, the officers and boards control the day-to-day spending, including political spending.  According to Stevens, the shareholders have few options, giving them "virtually nonexistent" recourse for opposing a corporation's political spending.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_24-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  Furthermore, most shareholders use investment intermediaries, such as mutual funds or pensions, and by the time a shareholder may find out about a corporation's political spending and try to object, the damage is done and the shareholder has funded disfavored speech.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conclusion">Conclusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Conclusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4><p>
Stevens concluded his dissent by writing:<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At bottom, the Court's opinion is thus a rejection of the common sense of the American people, who have recognized a need to prevent corporations from undermining self government since the founding, and who have fought against the distinctive corrupting potential of corporate electioneering since the days of Theodore Roosevelt. It is a strange time to repudiate that common sense. While American democracy is imperfect, few outside the majority of this Court would have thought its flaws included a dearth of corporate money in politics.<sup id="cite_ref-TNY20120521_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TNY20120521-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_developments">Subsequent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Subsequent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The decision was highly controversial and remains a subject of widespread public discussion.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-penguinrandomhouse.com_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penguinrandomhouse.com-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> There was a wide range of reactions to the case from politicians, academics, attorneys, advocacy groups and journalists.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support">Support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Politicians">Politicians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Politicians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4><p>
Senate Minority Leader <a href="/wiki/Mitch_McConnell" title="Mitch McConnell">Mitch McConnell</a>, a plaintiff in the earlier related decision <i><a href="/wiki/McConnell_v._FEC" title="McConnell v. FEC">McConnell v. FEC</a></i>, said:<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-politico2010-01-21_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politico2010-01-21-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For too long, some in this country have been deprived of full participation in the political process. With today’s monumental decision, the Supreme Court took an important step in the direction of restoring the First Amendment rights of these groups by ruling that the Constitution protects their right to express themselves about political candidates and issues up until Election Day. By previously denying this right, the government was picking winners and losers. Our democracy depends upon free speech, not just for some but for all.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Republican campaign consultant <a href="/wiki/Ed_Rollins" title="Ed Rollins">Ed Rollins</a> opined that the decision adds transparency to the election process and will make it more competitive.<sup id="cite_ref-Rollins2010_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollins2010-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Advocacy_groups">Advocacy groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Advocacy groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_(organization)" title="Citizens United (organization)">Citizens United</a>, the group filing the lawsuit, said, "Today's U.S. Supreme Court decision allowing Citizens United to air its documentary films and advertisements is a tremendous victory, not only for Citizens United but for every American who desires to participate in the political process."<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> During litigation, Citizens United had support from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="United States Chamber of Commerce">United States Chamber of Commerce</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Rifle_Association" title="National Rifle Association">National Rifle Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stohr_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stohr-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Campaign finance attorney Cleta Mitchell, who had filed an <a href="/wiki/Amicus_curiae" title="Amicus curiae">amicus curiae</a> brief on behalf of two advocacy organizations opposing the ban, wrote that "The Supreme Court has correctly eliminated a constitutionally flawed system that allowed media corporations (e.g., The Washington Post Co.) to freely disseminate their opinions about candidates using corporate treasury funds, while denying that constitutional privilege to Susie's Flower Shop Inc.&#160;... The real victims of the corporate expenditure ban have been nonprofit advocacy organizations across the political spectrum."<sup id="cite_ref-WP-opinions_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP-opinions-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heritage_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heritage Foundation">Heritage Foundation</a> fellow <a href="/wiki/Hans_A._von_Spakovsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Hans A. von Spakovsky">Hans A. von Spakovsky</a>, a former Republican member of the Federal Election Commission, said "The Supreme Court has restored a part of the First Amendment that had been unfortunately stolen by Congress and a previously wrongly-decided ruling of the court."<sup id="cite_ref-Dinan2010_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dinan2010-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Libertarian <a href="/wiki/Cato_Institute" title="Cato Institute">Cato Institute</a> analysts John Samples and Ilya Shapiro wrote that restrictions on advertising were based on the idea "that corporations had so much money that their spending would create vast inequalities in speech that would undermine democracy". They continued, "To make campaign spending equal or nearly so, the government would have to force some people or groups to spend less than they wished. And equality of speech is inherently contrary to protecting speech from government restraint, which is ultimately the heart of American conceptions of free speech."<sup id="cite_ref-Samples2010_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samples2010-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_Liberties_Union" title="American Civil Liberties Union">American Civil Liberties Union</a> filed an amicus brief that supported the decision,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> saying that "section 203 should now be struck down as facially unconstitutional", though membership was split over the implications of the ruling and its board sent the issue to its special committee on campaign finance for further consideration.<sup id="cite_ref-Sun-ACLU_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sun-ACLU-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> On March 27, 2012, the ACLU reaffirmed its stance in support of the Supreme Court's <i>Citizens United</i> ruling.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics_and_attorneys">Academics and attorneys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Academics and attorneys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bradley_A._Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="Bradley A. Smith">Bradley A. Smith</a>, professor of law at <a href="/wiki/Capital_University_Law_School" title="Capital University Law School">Capital University Law School</a>, former chairman of the FEC, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Competitive_Politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Center for Competitive Politics">Center for Competitive Politics</a> and a leading proponent of deregulation of campaign finance, wrote that the major opponents of political free speech are "incumbent politicians" who "are keen to maintain a chokehold on such speech". Empowering "small and midsize corporations—and every incorporated mom-and-pop falafel joint, local firefighters' union, and environmental group—to make its voice heard" frightens them.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith2010_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2010-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In response to statements by President Obama and others that the ruling would allow foreign entities to gain political influence through U.S. subsidiaries, Smith pointed out that the decision did not overturn the ban on political donations by foreign corporations and the prohibition on any involvement by foreign nationals in decisions regarding political spending by U.S. subsidiaries, which are covered by other parts of the law.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith2010a_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2010a-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Campaign finance expert Jan Baran, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Federal_Ethics_Law_Reform" title="Commission on Federal Ethics Law Reform">Commission on Federal Ethics Law Reform</a>, agreed with the decision, writing that "The history of campaign finance reform is the history of incumbent politicians seeking to muzzle speakers, any speakers, particularly those who might publicly criticize them and their legislation. It is a lot easier to legislate against unions, gun owners, 'fat cat' bankers, health insurance companies and any other industry or 'special interest' group when they can't talk back."  Baran further noted that in general <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservatives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libertarians" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarians">libertarians</a> praised the ruling's preservation of the First Amendment and freedom of speech, but that <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">liberals</a> and campaign finance reformers criticized it as greatly expanding the role of corporate money in politics.<sup id="cite_ref-Baran_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baran-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Attorney Kenneth Gross, former associate general counsel of the FEC, wrote that corporations relied more on the development of long-term relationships, political action committees and personal contributions, which were not affected by the decision. He held that while trade associations might seek to raise funds and support candidates, corporations which have "signed on to transparency agreements regarding political spending" may not be eager to give.<sup id="cite_ref-WP-opinions_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP-opinions-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The New York Times</i> asked seven academics to opine on how corporate money would reshape politics as a result of the court's decision.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTdebate_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTdebate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Three of the seven wrote that the effects would be minimal or positive: Christopher Cotton, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Miami" title="University of Miami">University of Miami</a> School of Business assistant professor of economics, wrote that "There may be very little difference between seeing eight ads or seeing nine ads (compared to seeing one ad or two). And, voters recognize that richer candidates are not necessarily the better candidates, and in some cases, the benefit of running more ads is offset by the negative signal that spending a lot of money creates.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTdebate_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTdebate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Volokh" title="Eugene Volokh">Eugene Volokh</a>, a professor of law at UCLA, stated that the "most influential actors in most political campaigns" are media corporations which "overtly editorialize for and against candidates, and also influence elections by choosing what to cover and how to cover it". Holding that corporations like <a href="/wiki/Exxon" title="Exxon">Exxon</a> would fear alienating voters by supporting candidates, the decision really meant that voters would hear "more messages from more sources".<sup id="cite_ref-NYTdebate_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTdebate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Joel Gora, a professor at <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Law_School" title="Brooklyn Law School">Brooklyn Law School</a> who had previously argued the case of <i>Buckley v. Valeo</i> on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_Liberties_Union" title="American Civil Liberties Union">American Civil Liberties Union</a>, said that the decision represented "a great day for the First Amendment" writing that the Court had "dismantled the First Amendment 'caste system' in election speech".<sup id="cite_ref-NYTdebate_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTdebate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Journalists">Journalists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Journalists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Editorial Board of the <i><a href="/wiki/San_Antonio_Express-News" title="San Antonio Express-News">San Antonio Express-News</a></i> criticized McCain–Feingold's exception for media corporations from the ban on corporate electioneering, writing that it "makes no sense" that the paper could make endorsements up until the day of the election but advocacy groups could not. "While the influence of money on the political process is troubling and sometimes corrupting, abridging political speech is the wrong way to counterbalance that influence."<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010a_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Anthony Dick in <i><a href="/wiki/National_Review" title="National Review">National Review</a></i> countered a number of arguments against the decision, asking rhetorically, "is there something uniquely harmful and/or unworthy of protection about political messages that come from corporations and unions, as opposed to, say, rich individuals, persuasive writers, or charismatic demagogues?" He noted that "a recent Gallup poll shows that a majority of the public actually agrees with the Court that corporations and unions should be treated just like individuals in terms of their political-expenditure rights".<sup id="cite_ref-Dick2010_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dick2010-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> A Gallup poll taken in October 2009 and released soon after the decision showed 57&#160;percent of those surveyed agreed that contributions to political candidates are a form of free speech and 55&#160;percent agreed that the same rules should apply to individuals, corporations and unions. Sixty-four percent of Democrats and Republicans believed campaign donations are a form of free speech.<sup id="cite_ref-Fabian2010_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fabian2010-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i> editorial board member Steve Chapman wrote "If corporate advocacy may be forbidden as it was under the law in question, it's not just Exxon Mobil and Citigroup that are rendered mute. Nonprofit corporations set up merely to advance goals shared by citizens, such as the American Civil Liberties Union and the National Rifle Association, also have to put a sock in it. So much for the First Amendment goal of fostering debate about public policy."<sup id="cite_ref-Chapman2010_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman2010-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Politicians_2">Politicians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Politicians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> stated that the decision "gives the special interests and their lobbyists even more power in Washington—while undermining the influence of average Americans who make small contributions to support their preferred candidates".<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010b_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010b-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Obama later elaborated in his weekly radio address saying, "this ruling strikes at our democracy itself" and "I can't think of anything more devastating to the public interest".<sup id="cite_ref-Superville2010_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Superville2010-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> On January 27, 2010, Obama further condemned the decision during the <a href="/wiki/2010_State_of_the_Union_Address" title="2010 State of the Union Address">2010 State of the Union Address</a>, stating that, "Last week, the Supreme Court reversed a century of law<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_f_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_f-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> to open the floodgates for special interests—including foreign corporations—to spend without limit in our elections. Well, I don't think American elections should be bankrolled by America's most powerful interests, or worse, by foreign entities." On television, the camera shifted to a shot of the SCOTUS judges in the front row directly in front of the President while he was making this statement, and Justice Samuel Alito was frowning, shaking his head side to side while mouthing the words "Not true".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Democratic Senator <a href="/wiki/Russ_Feingold" title="Russ Feingold">Russ Feingold</a>, a lead sponsor of the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act</a>, stated "This decision was a terrible mistake.
