Presented with a relatively narrow legal issue, the Supreme Court chose to roll back laws that have limited the role of corporate money in federal elections since Teddy Roosevelt was president."<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States_representative" class="mw-redirect" title="United States representative">Representative</a> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Grayson" title="Alan Grayson">Alan Grayson</a>, a Democrat, stated that it was "the worst Supreme Court decision since the <i><a href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_v._Sandford" title="Dred Scott v. Sandford">Dred Scott case</a></i>, and that the court had opened the door to political bribery and corruption in elections to come.<sup id="cite_ref-Baumann2010_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baumann2010-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Democratic congresswoman <a href="/wiki/Donna_Edwards" title="Donna Edwards">Donna Edwards</a>, along with constitutional law professor and Maryland Democratic State Senator <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Raskin" title="Jamie Raskin">Jamie Raskin</a>, have advocated petitions to reverse the decision by means of constitutional amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-PubRec_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PubRec-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Rep. <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Boswell" title="Leonard Boswell">Leonard Boswell</a> introduced legislation to amend the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-II_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-II-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> also called for an Amendment to overrule the decision.<sup id="cite_ref-Crabtree2010_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crabtree2010-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> On December 8, 2011, Senator <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a> proposed the Saving American Democracy Amendment, which would reverse the court's ruling.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>, co-crafter of the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Bipartisan_Campaign_Reform_Act" title="Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act">Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act</a> and the party's 2008 presidential nominee, said "there's going to be, over time, a backlash ... when you see the amounts of union and corporate money that's going to go into political campaigns".<sup id="cite_ref-Amick_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amick-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> McCain was "disappointed by the decision of the Supreme Court and the lifting of the limits on corporate and union contributions" but not surprised by the decision, saying that "It was clear that Justice Roberts, Alito and Scalia, by their very skeptical and even sarcastic comments, were very much opposed to BCRA."<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/Olympia_Snowe" title="Olympia Snowe">Olympia Snowe</a> opined that "Today's decision was a serious disservice to our country."<sup id="cite_ref-United2010_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United2010-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although federal law after <i>Citizens United v. Federal Election Commission</i> still prohibited corporate contributions to all political parties, Sanda Everette, co-chair of the Green Party, stated that "The ruling especially hurts the ability of parties that don't accept corporate contributions, like the Green Party, to compete." Another Green Party officer, <a href="/wiki/Rich_Whitney" title="Rich Whitney">Rich Whitney</a>, stated "In a transparently political decision, a majority of the US Supreme Court overturned its own recent precedent and paid tribute to the giant <a href="/wiki/Corporatocracy" title="Corporatocracy">corporate interests</a> that already wield tremendous power over our political process and political speech."
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a> condemned the ruling,<sup id="cite_ref-Nader2010_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nader2010-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> saying that "With this decision, corporations can now directly pour vast amounts of corporate money, through independent expenditures, into the electoral swamp already flooded with corporate campaign PAC contribution dollars." He called for shareholder resolutions asking company directors to pledge not to use company money to favor or oppose electoral candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-Nader2010a_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nader2010a-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pat_Choate" title="Pat Choate">Pat Choate</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Reform_Party_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Reform Party of the United States of America">Reform Party</a> candidate for Vice President, stated, "The court has, in effect, legalized foreign governments and foreign corporations to participate in our electoral politics."<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010c_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010c-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Senator Bernie Sanders</a>, a contender in the 2016 Democratic Primary, has filed a constitutional amendment to overturn the Supreme Court's Decision.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Further, both Sanders and Hillary Clinton said that, if they were elected, they would only have appointed Supreme Court Justices who were committed to the repeal of Citizens United.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In September 2015, Sanders said that "the foundations of American Democracy are being undermined" and called for sweeping campaign finance reform.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Sanders repeated such calls in the years since.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International">International</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: International">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Janez_Lenar%C4%8Di%C4%8D" title="Janez Lenarčič">Janez Lenarčič</a>, speaking for the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Office_for_Democratic_Institutions_and_Human_Rights" title="Office for Democratic Institutions and Human Rights">Office for Democratic Institutions and Human Rights</a> (which has overseen over 150&#160;elections) said the ruling may adversely affect the organization's two commitments of "giving voters a genuine choice and giving candidates a fair chance" in that "it threatens to further marginalize candidates without strong financial backing or extensive personal resources, thereby in effect narrowing the political arena".<sup id="cite_ref-Httpwwwosceorgodihr2010_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Httpwwwosceorgodihr2010-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics_and_attorneys_2">Academics and attorneys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Academics and attorneys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="rquote pullquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">

<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“
</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">Money isn't speech and corporations aren't people.
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;<a href="/wiki/David_Kairys" title="David Kairys">David Kairys</a><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The constitutional law scholar <a href="/wiki/Laurence_H._Tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Laurence H. Tribe">Laurence H. Tribe</a> wrote that the decision "marks a major upheaval in First Amendment law and signals the end of whatever legitimate claim could otherwise have been made by the Roberts Court to an incremental and minimalist approach to constitutional adjudication, to a modest view of the judicial role vis-à-vis the political branches, or to a genuine concern with adherence to precedent" and pointed out, "Talking about a business corporation as merely another way that individuals might choose to organize their association with one another to pursue their common expressive aims is worse than unrealistic; it obscures the very real injustice and distortion entailed in the phenomenon of some people using other people's money to support candidates they have made no decision to support, or to oppose candidates they have made no decision to oppose."<sup id="cite_ref-Tribe2010_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tribe2010-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Former Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Day_O%27Connor" title="Sandra Day O&#39;Connor">Sandra Day O'Connor</a>, whose opinions had changed from dissenting in <i>Austin v. Michigan State Chamber of Commerce</i> to co-authoring (with Stevens) the majority opinion in <i>McConnell v. Federal Election Commission</i> twelve years later, criticized the decision only obliquely, but warned, "In invalidating some of the existing checks on campaign spending, the majority in <i>Citizens United</i> has signaled that the problem of campaign contributions in judicial elections might get considerably worse and quite soon."<sup id="cite_ref-Liptak2010_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liptak2010-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Richard_L._Hasen" title="Richard L. Hasen">Richard L. Hasen</a>, professor of <a href="/wiki/Election_law" title="Election law">election law</a> at <a href="/wiki/Loyola_Law_School" title="Loyola Law School">Loyola Law School</a>, argued that the ruling "is activist, it increases the dangers of corruption in our political system and it ignores the strong tradition of American political equality". He also described Justice Kennedy's "specter of blog censorship" as sounding more like "the rantings of a right-wing talk show host than the rational view of a justice with a sense of political realism".<sup id="cite_ref-hasenslate_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hasenslate-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kathleen M. Sullivan, professor at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Law_School" title="Stanford Law School">Stanford Law School</a> and Steven J. Andre, adjunct professor at Lincoln Law School, argued that two different visions of freedom of speech exist and clashed in the case.  An egalitarian vision skeptical of the power of large agglomerations of wealth to skew the political process conflicted with a libertarian vision skeptical of government being placed in the role of determining what speech people should or should not hear.<sup id="cite_ref-sullivanspeech_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sullivanspeech-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-andrespeech_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andrespeech-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Wayne Batchis, Professor at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Delaware" title="University of Delaware">University of Delaware</a>, in contrast, argues that the Citizens United decision represents a misguided interpretation of the non-textual <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">freedom of association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The four other scholars of the seven writing in the <a href="#Academics_and_attorneys">aforementioned</a> <i>The New York Times</i> article were critical.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTdebate_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTdebate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_L._Hasen" title="Richard L. Hasen">Richard L. Hasen</a>, Distinguished Professor of <a href="/wiki/Election_law" title="Election law">election law</a> at <a href="/wiki/Loyola_Law_School" title="Loyola Law School">Loyola Law School</a> argued differently from his Slate article above, concentrating on the "inherent risk of corruption that comes when someone spends independently to try to influence the outcome of judicial elections", since judges are less publicly accountable than elected officials. Heather K. Gerken, Professor of Law at <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale Law School</a> wrote that "The court has done real damage to the cause of reform, but that damage mostly came earlier, with decisions that made less of a splash." <a href="/wiki/Michael_Waldman" title="Michael Waldman">Michael Waldman</a>, director of the <a href="/wiki/Brennan_Center_for_Justice" title="Brennan Center for Justice">Brennan Center for Justice</a> at <a href="/wiki/N.Y.U._School_of_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="N.Y.U. School of Law">N.Y.U. School of Law</a>, opined that the decision "matches or exceeds <i><a href="/wiki/Bush_v._Gore" title="Bush v. Gore">Bush v. Gore</a></i> in ideological or partisan overreaching by the court", explaining how "Exxon or any other firm could spend Bloomberg-level sums in any congressional district in the country against, say, any congressman who supports climate change legislation, or health care, etc." and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Wertheimer" title="Fred Wertheimer">Fred Wertheimer</a>, founder and president of <a href="/wiki/Democracy_21" title="Democracy 21">Democracy 21</a> considered that "Chief Justice Roberts has abandoned the illusory public commitments he made to 'judicial modesty' and 'respect for precedent' to cast the deciding vote for a radical decision that profoundly undermines our democracy," and that "Congress and presidents past have recognized this danger and signed numerous laws over the years to prevent this kind of corruption of our government."<sup id="cite_ref-NYTdebate_56-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTdebate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">TIME</a></i> Magazine survey of over 50 law professors, Richard Delgado (<a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama_Law_School" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Alabama Law School">University of Alabama</a>), <a href="/wiki/Cass_Sunstein" title="Cass Sunstein">Cass Sunstein</a> (<a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard</a>), and Jenny Martinez (<a href="/wiki/Stanford_Law_School" title="Stanford Law School">Stanford</a>) all listed <i>Citizens United</i> as the “worst Supreme Court decision since 1960,” with Sunstein noting that the decision is “undermining our system of democracy itself.”<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Journalists_2">Journalists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Journalists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> stated in an editorial, "The Supreme Court has handed lobbyists a new weapon. A lobbyist can now tell any elected official: if you vote wrong, my company, labor union or interest group will spend unlimited sums explicitly advertising against your re-election."<sup id="cite_ref-Kirkpatrick_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkpatrick-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Alter" title="Jonathan Alter">Jonathan Alter</a> called it the "most serious threat to American democracy in a generation".<sup id="cite_ref-Alter_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alter-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_Science_Monitor" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Science Monitor">Christian Science Monitor</a></i> wrote that the Court had declared "outright that corporate expenditures cannot corrupt elected officials, that influence over lawmakers is not corruption, and that appearance of influence will not undermine public faith in our democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_j_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_j-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_leaders">Business leaders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Business leaders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Cohen_(businessman)" title="Ben Cohen (businessman)">Ben Cohen</a>, the co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Ben_%26_Jerry%27s" title="Ben &amp; Jerry&#39;s">Ben &amp; Jerry's</a> ice cream, founded <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Stampede" title="Stamp Stampede">Stamp Stampede</a>, a sustained protest to demonstrate widespread support for a proposed constitutional amendment to overturn <i>Citizens United</i>. The campaign encourages people to rubber stamp messages such as "Not To Be Used for Bribing Politicians" on paper currency. In 2014, Cohen told <i><a href="/wiki/Salon_(website)" title="Salon (website)">Salon</a></i>, "As long as the Supreme Court rules money is speech, corporations and the wealthy are using it by giving piles of it to politicians to pass or not pass laws that they want. Now, the rest of the people, [those] who don't have that money, can actually make their voice heard by using money to stamp a message out."<sup id="cite_ref-Isquith_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isquith-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">Media coverage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Media coverage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_blogs">Political blogs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Political blogs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most blogs avoided the theoretical aspects of the decision and focused on more personal and dramatic elements, including the <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>–<a href="/wiki/Samuel_Alito" title="Samuel Alito">Samuel Alito</a> face-off during the President's State of the Union address.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> There, President Obama argued that the decision "reversed a century of law" (the federal ban on corporate contributions dates back to the 1907 <a href="/wiki/Tillman_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Tillman Act">Tillman Act</a>, and the ban on union and corporate expenditures <a href="/wiki/Labor_Management_Relations_Act_of_1947#Campaign_expenditures" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor Management Relations Act of 1947">dates from 1947</a>) and that it would allow "foreign corporations to spend without limits in our elections", during which Justice Alito, in the audience, perceptibly mouthed the words "not true". This event received extensive comment from political bloggers, with a substantial amount of the coverage concentrated on whether or not foreign corporations would be able to make substantial political contributions in US elections. In the opinion, the Court had specifically indicated it was not overturning the ban on foreign contributions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinion_polls">Opinion polls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Opinion polls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png/200px-Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png/300px-Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png/400px-Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png 2x" data-file-width="616" data-file-height="771" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Citizens_United_v._Federal_Election_Commission_Poll_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>ABC-Washington Post poll results.</div></div></div>
<p>An ABC–<i>The Washington Post</i> poll conducted February 4–8, 2010, showed that 80% of those surveyed opposed (and 65% strongly opposed) the <i>Citizens United</i> ruling, which the poll described as saying "corporations and unions can spend as much money as they want to help political candidates win elections". Additionally, 72% supported "an effort by Congress to reinstate limits on corporate and union spending on election campaigns". The poll showed large majority support from Democrats, Republicans and independents.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_k_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_k-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ref_l_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_l-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ref_m_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_m-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Gallup_Poll" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallup Poll">Gallup Poll</a> conducted in October 2009, after oral argument, but released after the Supreme Court released its opinion, found that 57&#160;percent of those surveyed "agreed that money given to political candidates is a form of free speech" and 55&#160;percent agreed that the "same rules should apply to individuals, corporations and unions". In the same poll, however, respondents by 52% to 41% prioritized limits on campaign contributions over protecting rights to support campaigns and 76% thought the government should be able to place limits on corporation or union donations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_n_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_n-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ref_o_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_o-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Separate polls by various conservative organizations, including the plaintiff Citizens United and the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Competitive_Politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Center for Competitive Politics">Center for Competitive Politics</a>, found support for the decision.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_p_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_p-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In particular, the Center for Competitive Politics poll<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_q_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_q-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> found that 51% of respondents believed that Citizens United should have a right to air ads promoting <i>Hillary: The Movie.</i> The poll also found that only 22 percent had heard of the case.
</p><p>Polling conducted by Ipsos in August 2017 found that 48% of Americans oppose the decision and 30% support it, with the remainder having no opinion. It also found that 57% percent of Americans, favored "limits on the amount of money super PACs can raise and spend".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_court_rulings">Further court rulings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Further court rulings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Campaign_finance_evolution_in_2010" title="Campaign finance evolution in 2010">Campaign finance evolution in 2010</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SpeechNow_v._FEC"><i>SpeechNow v. FEC</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: SpeechNow v. FEC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/SpeechNOW_v._FEC" class="mw-redirect" title="SpeechNOW v. FEC">SpeechNOW v. FEC</a></div>
<p>SpeechNow is a nonprofit, unincorporated association organized as a section 527 entity under the U.S. Internal Revenue Code. The organization was formed by individuals who seek to pool their resources to make independent expenditures expressly advocating the election or defeat of federal candidates. SpeechNow planned to accept contributions only from individuals, not corporations or other sources prohibited under the Federal Election Campaign Act. On February 14, 2008, SpeechNow and several individual plaintiffs filed a complaint in the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia challenging the constitutionality of the Federal Election Campaign Act provisions governing political committee registration, contribution limits and disclosure. The plaintiffs contended that the Act unconstitutionally restricts their association guaranteed under the First Amendment. By requiring registration as a political committee and limiting the monetary amount that an individual may contribute to a political committee, SpeechNow and the other plaintiffs asserted that the Act unconstitutionally restricted the individuals' freedom of speech by limiting the amount that an individual can contribute to SpeechNow and thus the amount the organization may spend. SpeechNow also argued that the reporting required of political committees is unconstitutionally burdensome.<sup id="cite_ref-FEC20121013_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEC20121013-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 26, 2010, the U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit ruled in <i>SpeechNow.org. v. FEC</i> that the contribution limits of 2 U.S.C. §441a were unconstitutional as applied to individuals' contributions to SpeechNow. The court also ruled that the reporting requirements of 2 U.S.C. §§432, 433 and 434(a) and the organizational requirements of 2 U.S.C. §431(4) and §431(8) can be constitutionally applied to SpeechNow.<sup id="cite_ref-FEC20121013_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEC20121013-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> A unanimous nine-judge panel of the United States Court of Appeals<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> struck down the federal limits on contributions to federal political committees that make only independent expenditures and do not contribute to candidates or political parties. This type of "independent expenditure committee" is inherently non-corruptive, the Court reasoned, and therefore contributions to such a committee can not be limited based on the government's interest in preventing political corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-CampaignLegalCenter20121013_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CampaignLegalCenter20121013-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In light of the Supreme Court's decision in <i>Citizens United v. FEC</i>, in which the Supreme Court held that the government has no anti-corruption interest in limiting independent expenditures, the appeals court ruled that "contributions to groups that make only independent expenditures cannot corrupt or create the appearance of corruption." As a result, the court of appeals held that the government has no anti-corruption interest in limiting contributions to an independent group such as SpeechNow. Contribution limits as applied to SpeechNow "violate the First Amendment by preventing [individuals] from donating to SpeechNow in excess of the limits and by prohibiting SpeechNow from accepting donations in excess of the limits." The court noted that its holding does not affect direct contributions to candidates, but rather contributions to a group that makes only independent expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-FEC20121013_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEC20121013-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The appeals court held that, while disclosure and reporting requirements do impose a burden on First Amendment interests, they "'impose no ceiling on campaign related activities'" and "'do not prevent anyone from speaking.'" Furthermore, the court held that the additional reporting requirements that the Commission would impose on SpeechNow if it were organized as a political committee are minimal, "given the relative simplicity with which SpeechNow intends to operate." Since SpeechNow already had a number of "planned contributions" from individuals, the court ruled that SpeechNow could not compare itself to "ad hoc groups that want to create themselves on the spur of the moment." Since the public has an interest in knowing who is speaking about a candidate and who is funding that speech, the court held that requiring such disclosure and organization as a political committee are sufficiently important governmental interests to justify the additional reporting and registration burdens on SpeechNow.<sup id="cite_ref-FEC20121013_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEC20121013-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_electoral_financing">Public electoral financing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Public electoral financing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/McComish_v._Bennett" class="mw-redirect" title="McComish v. Bennett">McComish v. Bennett</a></div>
<p>On June 27, 2011, ruling in the consolidated cases of <i>Arizona Free Enterprise Club's Freedom Club PAC v. Bennett</i> (No. 10-238) and <i><a href="/wiki/McComish_v._Bennett" class="mw-redirect" title="McComish v. Bennett">McComish v. Bennett</a></i> (No. 10-239), the Supreme Court deemed unconstitutional an Arizona law that provided extra taxpayer-funded support for office seekers who have been outspent by privately funded opponents or by independent political groups. A conservative 5–4 majority of justices said the law violated free speech, concluding the state was impermissibly trying to "level the playing field" through a public finance system. Arizona lawmakers had argued there was a compelling state interest in equalizing resources among competing candidates and interest groups.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Opponents said the law violated free-speech rights of the privately financed candidates and their contributors, inhibiting fundraising and spending, discouraging participation in campaigns and limiting what voters hear about politics.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters20110627_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters20110627-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Chief Justice John Roberts said in the court's majority opinion that the law substantially burdened political speech and was not sufficiently justified to survive First Amendment scrutiny.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters20110627_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters20110627-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a consequence of the decision, states and municipalities are blocked from using a method of public financing that is simultaneously likely to attract candidates fearful they will be vastly outspent and sensitive to avoiding needless government expense. "The government can still use taxpayer funds to subsidize political campaigns, but it can only do that in a manner that provides an alternative to private financing" said William R. Maurer, a lawyer with Institute for Justice, which represented several challengers of the law. "It cannot create disincentives."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The ruling meant the end of similar matching-fund programs in Connecticut, Maine and a few other places according to David Primo, a political science professor at University of Rochester who was an expert witness for the law's challengers.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_campaign-spending_limits">State campaign-spending limits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: State campaign-spending limits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_Tradition_Partnership,_Inc._v._Montana" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Tradition Partnership, Inc. v. Montana">Western Tradition Partnership, Inc. v. Montana</a></div>
<p>Despite the <i>Citizens United</i> ruling, in December 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Montana_Supreme_Court" title="Montana Supreme Court">Montana Supreme Court</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Western_Tradition_Partnership,_Inc._v._Attorney_General_of_Montana" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Tradition Partnership, Inc. v. Attorney General of Montana">Western Tradition Partnership, Inc. v. Attorney General of Montana</a>,</i> upheld that state's law limiting corporate contributions. Examining the history of corporate interference in Montana government that led to the Corrupt Practices Law, the majority decided that the state still had a compelling reason to maintain the restrictions. It ruled that these restrictions on speech were narrowly tailored and withstood strict scrutiny and thus did not contradict <i>Citizens United v. Federal Election Commission</i>.
</p><p>While granting permission to file a <a href="/wiki/Certiorari" title="Certiorari">certiorari</a> petition, the US Supreme Court agreed to stay the Montana ruling, although Justices Ginsburg and Breyer wrote a short statement urging the Court "to consider whether, in light of the huge sums of money currently deployed to buy candidate's allegiance, <i>Citizens United</i> should continue to hold sway".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In June 2012, over the dissent of the same four judges who dissented in <i>Citizens United</i>, the Court simultaneously granted certiorari and summarily reversed the decision in <i>American Tradition Partnership, Inc. v. Bullock</i>, 567, U.S. __ (2012).<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The Supreme Court majority rejected the Montana Supreme Court arguments in a two paragraph, twenty line per curiam opinion, stating that these arguments "either were already rejected in Citizens United, or fail to meaningfully distinguish that case."<sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120625_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120625-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The ruling makes clear that states cannot bar corporate and union political expenditures in state elections.<sup id="cite_ref-Atlantic20120625_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic20120625-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="McCutcheon_v._FEC"><i>McCutcheon v. FEC</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: McCutcheon v. FEC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/McCutcheon_v._FEC" title="McCutcheon v. FEC">McCutcheon v. FEC</a></div>
<p>In addition to limiting the size of donations to individual candidates and parties, the Federal Election Campaign Act also includes aggregate caps on the total amount that an individual may give to all candidates and parties. In 2012, Shaun McCutcheon, a <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> activist,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> sought to donate more than was allowed by the federal aggregate limit on federal candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> McCutcheon <i>et al</i> filed suit against the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission" title="Federal Election Commission">Federal Election Commission</a> (FEC).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, the US Supreme Court reversed a ruling of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_Columbia" title="United States District Court for the District of Columbia">DC District Court</a>'s dismissal of <i>McCutcheon v. FEC</i> and struck down the aggregate limits. The plurality opinion invalidated only the aggregate contribution limits, not limits on giving to any one candidate or party. The decisive fifth vote for McCutcheon came from Justice <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Thomas</a>, who concurred in the judgment on the grounds that all contribution limits are unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_responses">Legislative responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Legislative responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_impact">Legislative impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Legislative impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>The New York Times</i> reported that 24 states with laws prohibiting or limiting independent expenditures by unions and corporations would have to change their campaign finance laws because of the ruling.<sup id="cite_ref-Urbina2010_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urbina2010-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <i>Citizens United</i> and <i>SpeechNow.org</i> numerous state legislatures raised their limits on contributions to candidates and parties.<sup id="cite_ref-CCP2013_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2013-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> At the federal level, lawmakers substantially increased contribution limits to political parties as part of the 2014 budget bill.<sup id="cite_ref-Vogel121214_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel121214-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Such changes are widely perceived as efforts to place candidates and parties on something closer to equal footing with organizations making independent expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-Vogel121214_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel121214-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While many states and the federal government have raised contribution limits in response to <i>Citizens United</i>, proposals aimed at discouraging political spending, or providing for public financing of campaigns, have been less successful.
</p><p>Senator <a href="/wiki/Dick_Durbin" title="Dick Durbin">Dick Durbin</a> (D-IL) proposed that candidates who sign up small donors receive $900,000 in public money, but the proposal has not been acted on by Congress. Others proposed that laws on corporate governance be amended to assure that shareholders vote on political expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-Alter_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alter-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2010, Senator <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Schumer" title="Chuck Schumer">Charles E. Schumer</a> of New York, immediate past Chairman of the Democratic Senatorial Campaign Committee, and Representative <a href="/wiki/Chris_Van_Hollen" title="Chris Van Hollen">Chris Van Hollen</a> of Maryland, Chairman of the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee, outlined legislation aimed at undoing the decision.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt100211_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt100211-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>  In April 2010, they introduced such legislation in the Senate and House, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Eggen2010_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eggen2010-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> On June 24, 2010, H.R.5175 (The <a href="/wiki/DISCLOSE_Act" title="DISCLOSE Act">DISCLOSE Act</a>) passed in the House of Representatives but failed in the Senate. It would have required additional disclosure by corporations of their campaign expenditures.  The law, if passed, would also have prohibited political spending by U.S. companies with twenty percent or more foreign ownership, and by most government contractors.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_r_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_r-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The DISCLOSE Act included exemptions to its rules given to certain <a href="/wiki/Special_interests" class="mw-redirect" title="Special interests">special interests</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Rifle_Association" title="National Rifle Association">National Rifle Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_Retired_Persons" class="mw-redirect" title="American Association of Retired Persons">American Association of Retired Persons</a>. These gaps within the proposal attracted criticism from lawmakers on both political parties. "They are auctioning off pieces of the First Amendment in this bill... The bigger you are, the stronger you are, the less disclosure you have," said Republican Congressman Dan Lungren of California. Democratic Congressman Adam Schiff of California commented, "I wish there had been no carve-outs".<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010e_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010e-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
The bill was criticized as prohibiting much activity that was legal before <i>Citizens United</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithltr_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithltr-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The DISCLOSE Act twice failed to pass the U.S. Senate in the 111th Congress, in both instances reaching only 59 of the 60 votes required to overcome a unified Republican filibuster.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010f_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010f-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2010g_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2010g-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A scaled down version of the DISCLOSE Act was reintroduced in both the House and Senate in 2012 but did not pass.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Some have argued for a constitutional amendment to overturn the decision. Although the decision does not address "corporate personhood," a long-established judicial and constitutional concept,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> much attention has focused on that issue. Move to Amend, a coalition formed in response to the ruling,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> seeks to amend the Constitution to abolish <a href="/wiki/Corporate_personhood" title="Corporate personhood">corporate personhood</a>, thus stripping corporations of all rights under the Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In an online chat with web community Reddit, President Obama endorsed further consideration of a constitutional amendment and stated "Over the longer term, I think we need to seriously consider mobilizing a constitutional amendment process to overturn Citizens United (assuming the Supreme Court doesn't revisit it)".<sup id="cite_ref-WP20120829_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP20120829-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> He further elaborated that "Even if the amendment process falls short, it can shine a spotlight on the super-PAC phenomenon and help apply pressure for change."<sup id="cite_ref-WP20120829_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP20120829-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_reactions_by_state_and_local_lawmakers">Legislative reactions by state and local lawmakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Legislative reactions by state and local lawmakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Members of 16 state legislatures have called for a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_amendment" title="Constitutional amendment">constitutional amendment</a> to reverse the court's decision: California, Colorado, Connecticut, Delaware, Hawaii, Illinois, Maine, Maryland, Massachusetts, Montana, New Jersey, New Mexico, Oregon, Rhode Island, Vermont, and West Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of these are non-binding resolutions, but three states—Vermont, California, and Illinois—called for an Article V Convention to draft and propose a federal constitutional amendment to overturn <i>Citizens United</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, the Minnesota Senate passed a similar resolution, "Senate File No. 17," on May 2, 2013, but the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_House_of_Representatives" title="Minnesota House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> returned the measure to the General Calendar (meaning the measure did not pass) on May 15, 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Thirty-four states are needed to call an Article V convention.
</p><p>On a local level, Washington D.C. and 400 other municipalities passed resolutions requesting a federal constitutional amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since <i>Citizens United</i>, however, 13 states have actually raised their contribution limits.<sup id="cite_ref-CCP2013_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2013-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_impact">Political impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Political impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Citizens United</i> ruling "opened the door" for unlimited election spending by corporations, but most of this spending has "ended up being funneled through the groups that have become known as super PACs."<sup id="cite_ref-Mider-20-1-2016_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mider-20-1-2016-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>  Critics predicted that the ruling would "bring about a new era of corporate influence in politics," allowing companies and businesspeople to "buy elections" to promote their financial interests. Instead, large expenditures, usually through "Super PACS," have come from "a small group of billionaires", based largely on ideology. This has shifted power "away from the political parties and toward the ... donors themselves. In part, this explains the large number and variety of candidates fielded by the Republicans in 2016."<sup id="cite_ref-Mider-20-1-2016_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mider-20-1-2016-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Super_PACs">Super PACs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Super PACs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Citizens United v. Federal Election Commission</i> has often been credited for the creation of "<a href="/wiki/Political_Action_Committee#Super_PACs" class="mw-redirect" title="Political Action Committee">super PACs</a>", political action committees which make no financial contributions to candidates or parties, and so can accept unlimited contributions from individuals, corporations and unions. Certainly, the holding in <i>Citizens United</i> helped affirm the legal basis for super PACs by deciding that, for purposes of establishing a "compelling government interest" of corruption sufficient to justify government limitations on political speech, "independent expenditures, including those made by corporations, do not give rise to corruption or the appearance of corruption".<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It took another decision, by the U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit, <a href="/wiki/SpeechNOW.org_v._FEC" class="mw-redirect" title="SpeechNOW.org v. FEC"><i>Speechnow.org v. Federal Election Commission</i></a>, to actually authorize the creation of super PACs. While <i>Citizens United</i> held that corporations and unions could make independent expenditures, a separate provision of the Federal Election Campaign Act, at least as long interpreted by the Federal Election Commission, held that individuals could not contribute to a common fund without it becoming a PAC. PACs, in turn, were not allowed to accept corporate or union contributions of any size or to accept individual contributions in excess of $5,000. In <i>Speechnow.org</i>, the D.C. Circuit, sitting <a href="/wiki/En_banc" title="En banc">en banc</a>, held 9–0 that in light of <i>Citizens United</i>, such restrictions on the sources and size of contributions could not apply to an organization that made only independent expenditures in support of or opposition to a candidate but not contributions to a candidate's campaign.
</p><p><i>Citizens United</i> and <i>SpeechNOW</i> left their imprint on the <a href="/wiki/2012_United_States_presidential_election" title="2012 United States presidential election">2012 United States presidential election</a>, in which single individuals contributed large sums to "super PACs" supporting particular candidates. <a href="/wiki/Sheldon_Adelson" title="Sheldon Adelson">Sheldon Adelson</a>, the gambling entrepreneur, gave approximately fifteen million dollars to support <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a>. <a href="/wiki/Foster_Friess" title="Foster Friess">Foster Friess</a>, a Wyoming financier, donated almost two million dollars to <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a>’s super PAC. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Rove" title="Karl Rove">Karl Rove</a> organized super PACs that spent over $300 million in support of Republicans during the 2012 elections.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to indirectly providing support for the creation of super PACs, <i>Citizens United</i> allowed incorporated <a href="/wiki/501c4" class="mw-redirect" title="501c4">501(c)(4)</a> public advocacy groups (such as the National Rifle Association, the Sierra Club, and the group Citizens United itself) and trade associations to make expenditures in political races. Such groups may not, under the tax code, have a primary purpose of engaging in electoral advocacy. These organizations must disclose their expenditures, but unlike super PACs they do not have to include the names of their donors in their FEC filings. A number of partisan organizations such as Karl Rove's influential conservative <a href="/wiki/Crossroads_Grassroots_Policy_Strategies" class="mw-redirect" title="Crossroads Grassroots Policy Strategies">Crossroads Grassroots Policy Strategies</a> and the liberal 21st Century Colorado have since registered as tax-exempt 501(c)(4) groups (defined as groups promoting "social welfare") and engaged in substantial political spending.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> This has led to claims<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> of large secret donations,<sup id="cite_ref-penguinrandomhouse.com_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penguinrandomhouse.com-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> and questions about whether such groups should be required to disclose their donors. Historically, such non-profits have not been required to disclose their donors or names of members. See <i><a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Colored_People_v._Alabama" class="mw-redirect" title="National Association for the Advancement of Colored People v. Alabama">National Association for the Advancement of Colored People v. Alabama</a></i>.
</p><p>In an August 2015 essay in <i><a href="/wiki/Der_Spiegel" title="Der Spiegel">Der Spiegel</a></i>, Markus Feldkirchen wrote that the <i>Citizens United</i> decision was "now becoming visible for the first time" in federal elections as the super-rich have "radically" increased donations to support their candidates and positions via super PACs. Feldkirchen also said in the first six months of 2015 the candidates and their super PACs received close to $400 million: "far more than in the entire previous campaign." He opined that super-rich donating more than ever before to individual campaigns plus the "enormous" chasm in wealth has given the super-rich the power to steer the economic and political direction of the United States and undermine its democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-feldkirchen_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldkirchen-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In October 2015, <i>The New York Times</i> observed that just 158 super-rich families each contributed $250,000 or more, while an additional 200 families gave more than $100,000 for the 2016 presidential election. Both groups contributed almost half of the "early money" for candidates in the 2016 presidential election as of June 30, 2015 through channels like super PACs legalized by the Supreme Court's Citizens United decision.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Weakening_of_.22establishment.22_forces"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Weakening_of_&quot;establishment&quot;_forces">Weakening of "establishment" forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Weakening of &quot;establishment&quot; forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At least in the Republican Party, the Citizens United ruling has weakened the fund raising power of the Republican "establishment" in the form of the "three major" Republican campaign committees (<a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Congressional_Committee" title="National Republican Congressional Committee">National Republican Congressional Committee</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Senatorial_Committee" title="National Republican Senatorial Committee">National Republican Senatorial Committee</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Edsall_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edsall-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Columnist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_B._Edsall" title="Thomas B. Edsall">Thomas B. Edsall</a> notes that in 2008, "the last election before the Citizens United decision", the three campaign committees "raised six times" the money that "nonparty conservative organizations" did—$657.6 million vs. $111.9 million. By 2016 those party committees raised less than the independent groups—$652.4 million v. $810.4 million. Thus the new funding "freed candidates to defy" the party establishment, although not it seems, to move policy making away from traditional Republican priorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Edsall_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edsall-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_advantage">Republican advantage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citizens_United_v._FEC&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Republican advantage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Studies have shown that the Citizens United ruling gave Republicans an advantage in subsequent elections. One study by political scientists at University of Chicago, Columbia University and the London School of Economics found "that Citizens United increased the GOP’s average seat share in the state legislature by five percentage points. That is a large effect—large enough that, were it applied to the past twelve Congresses, partisan control of the House would have switched eight times. In line with a previous study, we also find that the vote share of Republican candidates increased three to four points, on average."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> A 2016 study in <i>the Journal of Law and Economics</i> found "that Citizens United is associated with an increase in Republicans’ election probabilities in state house races of approximately 4 percentage points overall and 10 or more percentage points in several states. We link these estimates to on-the-ground evidence of significant spending by corporations through channels enabled by Citizens United."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 23,</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Huffington+Post&amp;rft.atitle=President+Blasts+Supreme+Court+Over+Citizens+United+Decision&amp;rft.date=2010-01-23&amp;rft.aulast=Superville&amp;rft.aufirst=Darlene&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.huffingtonpost.com%2F2010%2F01%2F23%2Fobama-weekly-address-vide_n_434082.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACitizens+United+v.+FEC" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Ref_f-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ref_f_63-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This has been argued to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Tillman_Act_of_1907" title="Tillman Act of 1907">Tillman Act of 1907</a> and subsequent campaign finance laws like the 1947 <a href="/wiki/Taft-Hartley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Taft-Hartley Act">Taft-Hartley Act</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110605133214/http://www.brookings.edu/gs/cf/sourcebk/chap2.PDF">brookings.edu</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://blogs.wsj.com/law/2010/01/29/on-obama-v-alito-whos-right-heres-your-answer/tab/article/">blogs.wsj.com</a>. Others suggested that he simply was paraphrasing a sentence in Justice Stevens's dissent: "[t]he Court today rejects a century of history when it treats the distinction between corporate and individual campaign spending as an invidious novelty born of <i>Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce</i>, 494 U.S. 652 (1990)." <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jurist.law.pitt.edu/forumy/2010/02/constructive-criticism-presidential.php">jurist.law.pitt.edu</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100205054509/http://jurist.law.pitt.edu/forumy/2010/02/constructive-criticism-presidential.php">Archived</a> February 5, 2010, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>

An article about the beginnings of campaign finance law i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Tillman_Act_of_1907" title="Tillman Act of 1907">Tillman Act of 1907</a>, the Supreme Court case history regarding campaign finance before <i>Citizens United</i>, the court proceedings leading to the <i>Citizens United</i> ruling, and the aftermath of <i>Citizens United</i>.</li></ul>
